=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Meh. Too long for what it is. Battles are over complicated. The missions are fairly uninteresting.
--------------------------
Played again and came away with the same conclusions. Really don't see what the hype is about. It has some interesting ideas, but the execution is lacking. The tempo of the game is just all wrong. Military actions are too slow developing to be interesting. As the Empire, it does't feel like you're scouring the galaxy trying to find the Rebels. Since 75%+ of the missions are randomly drawn, single-use missions, player agency over the course of the game is minimized. It's all about figuring out how to do something with the missions you're dealt rather than to really set up something. Missions that reveal the vicinity of the Rebel base often also tip off the Rebel that you know, making a relocation of the base, if viable, obvious to both players. 

For a heavily thematic game, the narrative isn't there. It doesn't feel like a story is building. Characters appear where they're needed on that turn. There's no set up to them getting there. If I play a mission in system X, I know which of my opponent's leaders might appear there and because the choice is made to respond after seeing the mission, the narrative link of the opposing player having to choose before hand and thus creating some sort of story as to why the character is there is completely absent.

The things that both players might actually want to talk about to build the narrative are the things that are secret/hidden and can't be disclosed. If the Empire gets close to the base, the players cannot discuss to create any narrative. Missions are either an immediate success or failure, so they have no story arc. In certain limited circumstances you can chain multiple missions together to create some semblance of a story arc, but those are the exception rather than the rule and are generally unsatisfying anyway. Ultimately the mission resolution mechanic is completely flavorless:   each player counts a type of icon on their characters, rolls that many dice, and then counts the successes. Rinse and repeat for the rest. One mission feels exactly like every other. There's nothing to make me feel like I'm actually executing the different mission types. 

The core of the problem is that leaders magically appear wherever and resolve their mission. If, for example, you had to put a rebel leader on a ship and get him or her to the necessary location to even attempt the mission, then it might be interesting. You could have small rebel fleets passing through Imperial controlled systems with detection checks or something to see if the Rebels are discovered. It also seems like it needs some sort of baseline to roll against. Auto successes are boring. The two players' leaders that are committed should modify the baseline chance, but there should always be a chance of failure. Missions should not be randomly drawn but always available to the player to choose from. The more difficult missions should have prerequisites. Make the player complete several missions that leads to completing some big objective. For example, you could have some sort of Rebel spying mission that has a chance of recovering the death star plans, once the rebel player gets the plans, then they'd have to work to set up the conditions to exploit the weakness, then they'd finally be able to attempt to destroy it. By giving the player agency in actually achieving it instead of it being a matter of opportunity, the narrative is slowly built and its final destruction would be much more satisfying.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Small: Mini USA Yellow (95)

RATING: 8
Four full plays so far, two as Empire, two as Rebels. Rating based on that so it may change with more plays, likely settling out to 7. 

A fun game and very tense at times. Plays well with two, three or four, with minor mechanical changes per the rules. Slightly different feel with three or four since there is a limit on which leaders can be played in which order. I will probably home rule that either player can execute a mission on their turn, regardless of whose leader it is. That will allow for same tactical flexibility in missions as single player and prevent a player from having to pass for a few rounds.

I really like the unit construction in this game, I usually really dislike this part of most games as I want to be the general, not the quartermaster general. Here, units are built automatically on certain turns based on the systems are loyal to you. Perfect.

I was originally going to praise FFG on a not horrible rule book for a change. The players guide gives a good overview of how you play the game during each phase, with the deeper detail rules in a rules reference arranged alphabetically. This is great because one gets a good sense of how the game is actually played, but can easily lookup any questions that arise. HOWEVER FFG, put both sections in a single rulebook and for the love of all that is holy, DO NOT put a KEY RULE in a SIDEBAR in the players guide and not in the rules reference!!!!

A little concerned that the Rebel side Objectives do not align with what works play-wise (loyalty and sabotage) and force the Rebel player into combat before they are ready. And there are only five or so objectives in each level (I, II, III) so the Empire will have a pretty good idea of what the Rebels are up to or capable of. But I've only played them once, so perhaps viable strategies will reveal themselves. 

And despite the denials, some new objective and mission cards are a no-brainer for the inevitable expansions. I mean, just look at that box, it was made for expansion. I fit the entire game comfortably in a Star Wars LCG box, including a plano box.

RATING: N/A
4/7 Funagain games $59.99
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
There are enough reviews telling you about WHY the game is amazing, about why there is so much hype to this game. Truth be told, if you're a Star Wars fan, and you love complexity, this game will easily become a top 10 for you.

I will however, address some of the common themes for negative reviews, or the cons about this game.

"Clunky Mechanics/Bloated Combat/Long Combat/ Overcomplicated" Many people complain about the complexity or the clunky mechanics of the combat. But, in actuality it is the most simplified version of a more realistic version of combat scaling. Furthermore, it's not clunky even in the smallest bit. Combat is only "long/clunky" if you don't know the value of your choices, or don't appreciate what the Dev's did. It's completely balanced (in a way that gives the Empire a just edge) and if you've played more than 1 game, combat takes about 1-2 minutes per battle with larger battles taking 5-10 minutes simply because it's 20+ units. It's not hard, it's not clunky, but it might be long, but it is because you want to account for all tactical possibilities. And that's fun.

"It's all about Luck, Card Combinations, Dice Rolls." What game isn't? Anyone who ever makes this argument, ask them what their favorite game is. I guarantee you it has any one of the 3 combined. A reviewer said "It's all about Dice Rolls" and subsequently, one of their top games is Cube Quest. What? STR does have these elements, but most importantly, it's mitigated by STRATEGY. You alter to the meta of the game. You mitigate dice rolls with sheer numbers. Common sense people. I guarantee you any card combination, or outcome of battle, can be overcome with more than just luck, but strategy.

"Game is unbalanced." No, the game is actually perfectly balanced in my honest opinion, but there is a caveat. Experience goes a LONG way in this game, and whoever has played more, has an amazing edge to the game. Players might say one side or the other is overpowered, via mission cards or certain rebel objectives, but they're designed to be powerful. There is a way to counteract all the mission cards, or play around missions and objectives, and if you do this well, you're going to have to mitigate that power.

Negative values the game has I will acknowledge:

"Not a 3/4 player game" FFG tries to make this a 4 player game with admirals and generals, but being realistic here it doesn't really work out in an enjoyable experience. The only way I see this working, is if the players are more casual, or less strategic intensive and this is a great way to alleviate the burdens of many tasks to one individual. HOWEVER, I feel like most of the fun IS controlling everything in a game. Take for instance most RTS or Strategy games, you don't want to do all the macro and none of the micro or vice versa, otherwise you'll feel like it's a waste.

"The game is too long!" Yeah, it certainly is, I agree. Gametime easily hits 4+ hours in every run I've done, and that's with me feeling like the game is flying by pretty quick. But, I think it's worth it in my opinion. Certainly harder to do "in a pinch" though.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
[2018-01-13]
A War of the Ring élmény. Jól működő, asszimetrikus kétszemélyes játék a SW Lázadás. Ennek a témának se vagyok rajongója, így nem tudom mennyire áll, hogy ez a legjobb SW játék, azért én ezt nem biztos, hogy kijelenteném, bár a karakterek és az egyes küldetés lapok, a lázadók rejtőzködése, a birodalom hatalmas ereje mind erősen hozzájárul a beleéléshez. A fő mechanizmus, azaz, hogy nincsenek fix akciók, hanem kártyák adják őket és az egyes szereplők alkalmazásával aktiválódnak nagyon tetszett. Nagyon jó, hogy a talonban tartott arcok tudnak a másik fél akcióira reagálni, ezért fontos döntés, hogy kiket vetünk be egy-egy küldetés megoldása, kikkel támadunk és kiket hagyunk talonba a reagálásokra. Tetszett az eltérő cél is. Az első játékunk 1 órás volt, mert olyan hamar sikerült a birodalomnak egy véletlen kártyával beletenyerelnie a lázadók rendszerébe, de gyanús, hogy ez óriási szerencse és az első játék ismerkedő mivolta miatt sikerült ilyen gyorsra. A második játék már sokkal izgalmasabb volt, de még mindig a birodalmi oldalt éreztem erősebbnek. Teljesen más a két oldal játéka, a véletlenszerűen feljövő lapok miatt változatosak lehetnek a partik, de nem biztos, hogy jobban, mint a Gyűrűk Urában. Szóval nagyon hasonló élmény volt a két játék, ezért a pontszám is azonos. Ha a lázadókkal is sikerül majd játszanom emelkedhet a pont, jelenleg nagyon erős hetes, de azért a top6-os helyet erősen túlzásnak érzem.

RATING: 9.5
Enjoyed the asymmetric setup in the game: the Empire is a menace and the Rebels are on the run. Yet the game is deep, remains balanced, and on a perpetual knife-edge.

Star Wars theme really shines through. Rebels won one game with Luke drawing an ace shot against the Death Star. In another, Palpatine turned Leia to the dark side after the Death Star blew up Alderaan.

One minor negative: the combat system can slow down gameplay. Players have to take turns throwing dice.

The production quality of the miniatures and the game board is excellent. Unlike many other Star Wars games: everything you need is included in the box! No collectibles/gadgets removed from the game to be sold separately. In fact, the game is such a complete experience and so well balanced that I don't see much that could be added without diluting the base game (maybe another set of dice? combat revamp?).

RATING: 8
Game is good, will need further plays to evaluate more because my first play took about halfway through the game for everything to click.

Will probably have a lot of positive things to add later, but the negatives stand out to me more for now and are unlikely to change:

The combat is a bit of a weakness I think. The card play during combat is pretty boring, and I don't think the red/black system is terribly interesting either. So you're left with "just" dice. That's not always a bad thing, and I know a lot of people have been calling for simpler combat with FFG's games, but for such an epic game it'd be nice if the battles had a bit more to them. The combat is simplified compare to FFG games with great combat systems (Forbidden Stars, Middle Earth Quest, etc), but is still just convoluted enough that I want some more depth for the effort.

I also wish I felt the characters and their doings more. They seem like numbers and stats to me which seems wrong.

Finally, the cards -- there simply aren't enough. This applies to almost everything -- missions, objectives, etc.

(4 Design + 4 Enjoyment)

RATING: 9
This is it, the Star Wars game that I have always wanted.  Rebellion is grand scale depiction of the original Star Wars trilogy that captures the cinematic moments, epic conflict, and tension of the movies.  Components and art are the usual Fantasy Flight excellence, though much of the art is reused from their previous Star Wars games.  Gameplay is a mix of strategy, tactics, and dice rolling.  There is some serious strategic depth to Rebellion.  Gameplay time of 3-4 hours on the box is accurate.  The rules of not overly complex, so it is easy to learn and teach.  Combat is good, but not unique in any way.  The beauty of this game lies in its asymmetrical wins conditions and evocation of the original Star Wars films.  No fan of Star Wars or strategy conflict games should fail to play this one.

RATING: 10
I've played this game a few times now and I'm in love. I am biased in that I am a huge fan of the OT, but I feel that this game captures that theme perfectly.

As a rebel player, you are desperate and always feel like you're losing - and yet, when you pull out that incredible objective to win the game it's a momentous moment and you realise that you are playing for the guerilla tactic - hit and run techniques, inciting rebellions, picking and choosing your battles where possible. You aren't playing for board control or for supremacy, just to survive!

As an imperial player it's easy to assume that this will be easy. Your navies and armies outmatch theirs. Your strength in combat is usually unstoppable. But... as you slowly spread across the galaxy, constantly having the rebels undermine your every move, you begin to realise that your huge navies and armies aren't enough... probing systems or getting boots on the ground and still, you haven't found them. The clock is ticking, and you feel a lot less confident as you realise that it isn't about controlling the galaxy, it's about quelling this damn rebellion before they get too much traction.

Inside of all of this, there is so much narrative to be had. Leaders engaging in epic combats is just the least of it - Chewie leading a rebellion on his home planet only to be captured and frozen in carbonite! Han on the brink of capture only for Luke to swing in and save his friend! The empire maliciously captures one of your generals only to send him home - with a tracking beacon! 

You probably won't play out the events of the OT but the events that play out are stories that you and your friends create together as an alternate Star Wars story, and that's a great feeling. Combat is the only 'weakness' in this game as it does not feel as smooth as everything else, but it's still a working system that simulates a real battle in a simplified sense. 

A must have for a Star Wars nerd!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The most complete Star Wars experience around. Epic retelling of the original trilogy in unique ways every game. Win or lose, I love this experience. I have a friend whom I play regularly and I've won once out of 7 or 8 games, and yet I would still go back and play more with him because every time we have a funny story to recount about some ridiculously cinematic moment during the course of the session. 

Fantastic decision to buy this, if you are considering it!

RATING: 10
Star Wars in a box!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is a little hard to categorize.  I guess more than anything else it is an area control game with light skirmish combat.  But there is also political intrigue and hide-and-seek to be had as well.  One thing it absolutely is, is Star Wars.  There is certainly a lot of theme here from the detail of the miniatures to the locations events need to take place in.

The game itself isn't too terribly complicated in terms of overall structure.  Each round you assign leaders to missions, resolve missions and activate spaces on the board, then cleanup and do it all over again the next round.  The complexity and depth comes in with the breadth of the missions and actions that you can take.  The overall goal for the Empire players is to find and conquer the rebel base (or wipe it from the map with the Death Star).  The Rebels basically have to keep them from doing this long enough to garner enough support in the galaxy, this is done with private objective cards.

I liked the head-to-head gameplay a lot that involved a lot of intrigue and predicting and out-maneuvering of your opponent.  I also like the very different status and viewpoints of the two sides.  I played the Rebels in my first game and they are COMPLETELY outgunned.  However, some of the ways they can garner support are through combat, so that involves precisely picking your battles to find winnable ones because at the end of the day you just cannot compete with the Empire player in combat.  Planning in this game also very much involves competing with your opponent on a mental level.  Planning of missions is done by both players prior to resolving them and once you are in the resolution step you cannot go back and plan a new mission.  So some missions like rescuing a leader you may want to plan even though you currently don't have a leader captured in case they are during that round (because next round you would be short a leader and therefore also an action).  However a leader assigned to a mission cannot be used to oppose an opponents mission or activate a system even if that mission is not performed.  And especially from the Rebel point of view if the Empire player can get his Death Star in your hidden base space and fire it in the same turn, you lose.  You can always move your base but you have to plan it and do it BEFORE it is destroyed.  There's a lot of layers and nuance and that's the best part of the game.  In fact, I kind of thought I liked this game less than I did until I started writing about it and convinced myself how much I actually did enjoy and appreciate it.

Finally, the game looks amazing.  Especially the figures.  There are close to 200 figures in this game of everything from Storm Troopers to Death Stars both "fully operational" and under construction.  They all look just like the movies and really help with the immersion.  The map is a sprawling two-board affair filled with planets you remember from the flims.  Tattooine, Naboo, Hoth, Dagobah, etc.  It is a little disappointing to have so many well-made miniatures and have to use cardboard standees for the leaders but it is also more functional since they have a lot of printed information on them.  Overall a beautiful high-quality production for a reasonable price when you consider everything that comes in the box (although I personally received my copy as a gift, not a purchase).

I wrote just briefly ago I thought I liked this game less than I did and I think the reason for that is definitely the playtime.  Not simply the playtime but the playtime compared to expected playtime.  My first game took over 4 hours.  Which is reasonable when you consider the estimate is 3-4 and I was playing with a non-boardgamer, it was both our first times, rules were revisited frequently, and most of the cards had to be read and re-read to understand and remind ourselves what to do.  A further play might take closer to 3 than 4 which is more manageable but I do wish it was a bit shorter.  I don't inherently have an issue with longer games, I will and have played a game of Duel of Ages for 6 hours.  But I always judge games in terms of my desire to keep them on the shelf and I was very close to putting this one in the trade pile despite how great of a game objectively I think it is.  Because I have other 2-player only games that involve area control, skirmish battles, and mental duels.  But those other games also don't take 4 hours which is a hard time slot to fit a game into.  Especially when you consider setup itself can tack on another hour, even in this first game where we used a recommended setup, just finding spaces and placing the necessary figures on the board took quite a while and cleanup isn't a ton better.  So I worry about how often this game will get played.  But for all the  other games I have that do what this one does, it does one thing none of my others do as well.  Star Wars.

RATING: 9
Completely sensational game in every respect. The box is filled up with components up to edges, thanks to which one has the feeling that players will be clashing on a galactic level simply by unpacking the elements from the box.

The art on the board, cards and other playing pieces is fantastic, although the quality of the plastic playing pieces seem to be inferior when compared to those in Forbidden Stars.

The instructions in the rule book are very accessible and intuitive, and the compendium clarifies  individual situations which can present problems in the interpretation of the rules. But most importantly, Rebellion gives so much pleasure in playing as hardly any game. The asymmetry of the factions, the search for the rebel base, the controlled randomness and the attempt to counteract the unknown, covert actions of one's opponent; those elements keep one in tension and uncertainty all the time increasing the experience and fun.

Furthermore, the game focuses on the events known from episodes IV-VI and provides the players with the means of telling a different story each time, different to the one know from the films. This is another pro aspect of this game which puts it high on my list, as the replayability is ensured not only by the game's mechanics but rather through it's strong emphasis on story-telling with the possibility of creating countless new versions of the events from many a fan's favorite movies in the Star Wars franchise.

RATING: 10
This game is a real jewel.  I tend to not rate lengthy games extremely well; I always have HUGE expectations for long games since they need to be worth the chunk of my time they take.  Furthermore, I generally do not like 2 player games because I don't get much of a chance to play them, and I lost horribly on my first game, but I still came out liking this game a lot, that should show you just how good it is.

I am a major Star Wars nut and this captured the feeling of Star Wars so extraordinarily well.  The rebels are crazy lacking in tech and ships and the empire can pump our Star Destroyers like nothing, it is scary as hell to be the rebel player, but it makes it all the better putting you in that mindset.  If it sounds like it is imbalanced, I don't think it is.  The rebels aren't trying to defeat the empire, just hold out a certain number of turns before their base is discovered, and they can do a number of obnoxious hit and run missions to delay the empire, many of which are references or very believable things the rebels would be capable of.  Additionally you can do missions to turn luke into a Jedi while the empire can try and turn your own characters against you!  In my first game Luke became a jedi, mon mothma turned to the darkside and Luke vs Vader ended in a final showdown over my base.  I also made a failed attempt at blowing up the deathstar!  

It is rare when a games mechanics matches its theme, and even rarer when the game turns out to be good.  This does it all and remains amazing.  Definitely on my top 5 best games of all time.

RATING: 10
The perfect game for any Star Wars fan. As you are playing the missions you can easily imagine how it would look in the movies. The Imperial player uses their military might to hunt down the Rebel base, while the Rebel player relies on hit and run missions and diplomacy to slow the progress of the Empire. The asymmetry also contributes to the theme. The Rebel player feels hopeless on turn 1 because the Empire appears to have a huge advantage, but by turn 6 or 7 the Rebellion starts to see a path to victory. The four hour play time just flies by. This is one of those games where even if you lose, you have a lot of fun playing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I pan a lot of Fantasy Flight games as overly thematic with too much chrome and not enough gameplay. Not so with Star Wars Rebellion. Here FF turns its weaknesses into strengths. To do so, however, FF exercises restraint. The game amounts to a standard battle system layer on top of a giant game of cat and mouse. Or to put it another way, it's [thing=438]Scotland Yard[/thing] combined with a war game. This basic premise is the key to the game's success because it cleverly encapsulates the three original Star Wars movies. It all boils down to a question that is ever-frustrating for the Empire: "Where is that damn Rebel base!?" If the Empire finds and destroys the base, victory! On the other hand, if the Rebels outlast the Empire and end the game before the base is found, victory for them! Gameplay is asymmetrical, but of course it has to be, and it works.

What FF does right is leave well enough alone. Rather than complicate the game with unnecessary rules, they take a lesson from games like [thing=12333]Twilight Struggle[/thing]. The thematic details are all in the cards. Both players have mission cards they can play to take special actions (necessary if you hope to win). So instead of simply moving troops around on the board, you have to launch plans that involve a bit of setup and luck. In addition, the Rebel player has objective cards (goals that allow him to speed up the game and therefore bring him closer to victory). All you need to "feel" like you're playing Star Wars is right there in two systems: the basic premise of hide and seek and the player-cards. Well done, FF.

I only hope the Rebel and Empire sides are balanced enough for longterm enjoyment. There's no way to know now, but it's a concern I have with any asymmetrical game.

What's missing from a typical FF production are things like random events and unnecessary rules. That's not to say the game doesn't come close. There are leaders, which you must use to perform actions like moving your troops and carrying out special actions. Each leader has a host of stats that dictate how many you dice you roll in different circumstances. There is no reason to have so many different stats when just a few would do. Having so many options only serves to confuse players. Likewise, some of the cards contain additional rules that new players won't know about until the game surprises them. Knowing the cards and all their strategic implications gives an experienced player a serious advantage -- but this is a trade-off you make with any card-driven game. Neither of these criticisms are enough to sully the game, but I feel a bit of extra honing would have done the game well.

Finally, I have to mention FF's rules, which I historically can't stand. I love the new split rulebook approach, dividing the rules into a reference book (for experienced players) and a learning manual (for new players). Awesome job there. But like so many FF games, they play it fast and loose with terminology and fail to understand the importance of using precise and consistent language. This leads to rules ambiguity that could (and should) be avoided. I don't know why FF has so many problems with their terminology, but I'd love to see them put as much effort into their writing as they do their plastic miniatures and illustrations.

Overall, though, Star Wars: Rebellion is fun, tense, and interesting. That I'm a huge Star Wars fan helps, of course, but being a fan has never helped me enjoy a FF game in the past. The gameplay is the thing.

RATING: 8
Game says 3-4 hours, that's if you know what you're doing and you're not playing with an AP player.  That being said, I've enjoyed this game very much (and I'm not good at it, lost both times).
It really is Star Wars in a box.  One game the empire kept quelling my uprising and based on my actions, the empire had a good idea where my hidden base was and the death star approached Tatooine to destroy it.
Another game the empire kept gaining planets, but the Rebellion sabotaged production wherever it could and won some important battles.  The empire captured Luke and converted him to the dark side, but then the rebellion found the death star plans and destroyed it with 1 lone X-Wing and a lucky roll.
This is a game that tells stories.  All of the cards compliment each other.  Some are very specific, but the other side's cards will do that very thing that you are good at blocking.  Lots of back and forth.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
First Impression: Oh my, what an exciting game. This is THE wet dream of every star wars fan. Either commanding fleets of star Destroyers hunting down Rebel Scum or making daring strikes against the overwhelming empire to keep the hope up as the brave rebels this is the entire Star Wars spirit in one great boardgame, much more than any other board or card game has achieved ever before.

But is it good as a game? Does it hold for those that don't care for the Star Wars theme? And the odd answer is yes! This is space opera at its finest, with the focus on the opposing characters, each with his own strengths and weaknesses, and a gameplay that is both simple, elegant and completely absorbing. There is assymetrical gameplay here, also at its finest, just the right amount of economy thrown in, an amazing pacing, a ton of bluffing and outguessing and it creates an amazing story every time.

So is there anything wrong with it? Well, it is long, and even if you'll be absorbed by it and never feel the pass of time, you still need an afternoon to play this with full comfort. Other than that the only thing I can say is that as with all 2p skill based games this one will be an autoloss for new players that face veterans, but to the game's credit they will still last long enough to fully enjoy themselves and won't really realize their doom until the veteran moves for the killing blow(s).

RATING: 9
This game is a lot of fun. You have to be ready to play for three to four hours but it is worth it. The game does not drag and it is fun until the end.

Focusing on leaders as the main drive for all actions is what makes this game feel so epic. Star Wars is all about the characters and the actions of a few changing the Galaxy while the war rages around them. Star Wars: Rebellion captures that with a few very simple mechanics based around the use of each player's leaders. 

On a mechanical level the back and forth between each player is great fun. Trying to anticipate and counter your opponent is a very fun kind of stress. Every turn is full of meaningful decisions and derailed plans. You never have enough resources to do everything you want. You will always have to delay or forgo something that you want to do in favor of something else. It's great. This leaves a lot of replay-ability and shows that there is not just one best way to win for each side.

Decisions are not as meaningful in combat but the choices you make do matter. Whether or not to over assign damage, where to assign damage and how to play your cards are all can decide how a combat is won. There is a slight disjoint as you go from space combat to ground combat to space again when the natural instinct is to completely resolve all rounds in one before moving to the other, but you get used to it. I especially like the simplicity of the dice system for differentiating between light and heavy attacks what they are effective against.

The production system is simple and quick and makes it very clear what each planet is worth to either cause. The inclusion of remote systems that have no production really adds to mind games of the hidden base mechanic and your limited actions. The Imperial player wants to check these locations, but it always feels like it's a wasted action. It's a fantastic tension.

This game is full of different tensions. Do the Rebels want to deploy units around the base to protect it, or to be a distraction and pull Imperial units away from it? The Imperials want to spread out but must always keep forces together to not leave themselves open to Rebel uprisings and surprise attacks.

The team version of the rules is just as fun as the head to head. The additional complication of having to work out which order your leaders will need to activate in makes it shine.

A must for fans of boardgamers and  Star Wars. Even for someone not familiar with Star Wars this is an excellent game. It's just good. No movie knowledge required.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
It captures the theme of Star Wars with amazing components and game mechanics. One of the best 2 player games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
It's been said by a lot of other people but this really is Star Wars in a box. Every time I play this games it feels just like playing in my own Star Wars film.

The different sides feel really fun to play and are both equally stressful to play against. As the Rebels it's all about the mind games and keeping the Empire off your back. As the Empire, you're constantly trying to find out where the Rebels are hidden.

While the game touts being a 2-4 player game, it really is 2p only. The 4-player option chops the game in half and adds a clunky turn mechanic to the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
About [thing=187645][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=187645][/thing] is a heavily thematic game using area movement, hand management and some light bluffing. It is mostly made for 2 players, one playing the Empire, other the Rebels, but it can be played as a 4 players game, dividing the factions, much like in [thing=115746][/thing].

This is a heavy game, both literally (as the box is quite huge and there is plenty of components, cards, tokens and several miniatures), and figuratively, as though the main mechanics aren't hard to learn, there is plenty of special cases with their own set of rules or exceptions - and since this is a game with lots of secrecy in both sides, simply asking something during play can give a key information, making learning these little situations important, and, also, a hassle in first play.

The components, as expected, are all very nice: the miniatures are well done, the cards and tokens are standard, and the art works well, as it allows the use of characters from different media to participated in the same game.

However, it is in the translation of mechanics to theme that [thing=187645][/thing] shines the most: it brings a lot of Star Wars to the table - the setting is there, alongside with its characters, situations and overall feel.

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
The basics are: the Empire wants to find and conquer the Rebel base, and the Rebels want to fulfill objectives, in order to advance their influence, which makes the time for the Empire shorter, and also needs to delay the Empire as best as possible, while the Empire use mostly brute force in order to speed things up.

Both players have a hand of mission cards, and do use them, they must assign a Leader to each of them. Missions have requirements of ability, which restrict who you can send. Missions do all sort of things and the important to know right now is that some have the word "Attempt" and others the word "Resolve" - in the first case, the other side can send an unassigned Leader to oppose the opponent Leader, with both rolling the appropriated attribute. For the "Resolve" there is no opposing.

As I mentioned, while picking Leaders to send on missions, keeping some behind may also be wise, since these can be used in the hopes of trying to foil some actions done by the other side.

At the start of some rounds, players draw new Leaders and/or produce (planets give out ships or ground units for their controlling faction). Also, two new mission cards are drawn - there is a limit of 10 mission cards in hand. The Empire player draws two probe cards (these shows planets that the Rebels base isn't in - as the home world of the Rebels base is out of the deck). The Rebels player draw two Objective cards and chooses one to keep.

At the end of the round, the time token moves 1 space - when it meets the Rebel marker, the game is over, and the Rebels win. The Empire wins by fiding and conquering (or destroying) the Rebels base before the times runs out.

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
There are plenty of them, the main ones are:
- Which mission to use and which Leader to send to it;
- In which place to do a mission (as Leaders prevent units of the same side from moving from where they are);
- Which Leader (if any) to keep and possibly use to act against missions of the other side;
- Where to move units - important for both sides, but matters more for the Empire;
- Where to allocate new units;
- Picking new Leaders;
- For the Rebel's player: which Objectives to keep and how best to fulfill them.

The first two are the most relevant, as they direct many of the others. Keeping some Leaders behind can make the other side to rethink what they will do, or, at least, when, maybe trying to push the opponent to use a Leader that stayed behind sooner, maybe in a less important mission. It is also important And picking missions to do us the heart of the whole game.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Dripping with theme - it is the universe of Star Wars in a box;
- Excellent production value;
- Several decision points throughout the play;
- Great tension for both sides: with the Rebels advancing their goals and the Empire getting closer and more sure as time goes by.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- Convoluted rules, as there so many little rules for several special situations, making learning everything a chore;
- Combat isn't very exciting;
- There is an important learning curve, for both sides, which makes playing against a veteran (or newbie) a bad or lesser experience.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
Like in a game of cat and mouse, but the mouse is Jerry: you know both sides will get out with bruises. The Empire is stronger, yes, but its machine can be slowed down almost to a halting point due to diplomacy and sabotage, and at the same time making the Rebels stronger. However, there isn't t that beautiful dwindling hope the Free Peoples go through in [thing=115746][/thing], facing almost impossible odds, as the Shadow rarely falters in their great military power. But, yes, the Empire have the Global Universal FriendShip.

In the end, [thing=187645][/thing] works very well, specially in bringing the theme to the surface with its mechanics. There is so much to like: the options, and bluffing, the timming, the tug of war, the growing tension. Yet, I'm not the target audience for it: I usually avoid lenghty and confrontational games for 2 players, and I play to relax and these tend to give some stress to me. Also, the I'm neutral to the Star Wars universe: I know it well enough, as almost anyone breathing, so it is a hard sell to me a 3 to 5 hours game without the backing of the theme (and is why I like WotR so much). Still, I can understand the value and fun many see here, as I see both in WotR.  Therefore, it goes recommended.

RATING: 10
I can't play this game enough! People love the game because it feels like Starwars and it does. The rebels are pesky and annoying saboteurs. Meanwhile, the Empire is incredibly powerful! The two concerns people have are replayability and the combat system. As for replayability, there are so many different avenues to victory on both sides that you could play as the Empire dozens of times without getting bored or even trying the Rebel side! Do I focus on building my fleet? Exploring the board for their base? Interrogating and sending droids for intel? You can't do it all! Or as a Rebel, do I rush objective card? Build a powerful base? Where should I hide my base?!? There is just so much to do and try differently each time you play it - let alone the differences in leaders and mission cards you'll get each play! As for combat, it is a little clunky but there is still a lot of fun and strategy. The last match I played ended with an assault on the Rebel base where the Rebels survived with two Y-wings on their planet. Additionally, there is some strategy as to which troops you decide to build and how you acquire them throughout the board. Overall, an amazing game!

RATING: 8
The game is much more straight-forward than I expected.  Much more card play with a bit of combat thrown in, which was a nice surprise. Definitely as much theme as you can pour into a box.  

Stepping away from the Star Wars fanboy influence though, it does feel too long for what it is.  It starts to feel repetitive and the tension is so drawn out that it loses it's edge.  It's supposed to be a race to find the Rebel Base, but there are soooo many planets, and it takes soooo much time to move fleets around that it's almost like a nuisance rather than an urgent chase like in some cat and mouse games. 

There are also some clunky and lacking bits.  Using damage counters to keep track of who produced.  Using leaders as a mark to limit chaining movement.  Both felt a bit like makeshift fixes.  Wish it came with printable maps for the Imperial player to keep track of planets he's checked because I found constantly shuffling through probe cards to be a big problem.  I made several mistakes because I kept getting planets jumbled in my head.   Combat could fee like a chore and predetermined by card draw.

Personally, War of the Ring felt more like a memorable, epic experience.  I was surprised to discover that the 30 minute experience in Risk: Star Wars felt like a similar game in 1/10th the time.

I like a lot of what is going on here, I just wish it moved at a quicker pace and a bit less fiddly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[i]Preliminary rating after one play as the Empire:[/i]

Epic and fun. I really enjoyed playing this game. I love the asymmetric objectives, both sides really have to use completely different strategies (in fact, they play two totally different games). I think that the Rebel player has more fun things to do, the Empire has to focus on the logistics of movement and exploration a little too much.

There are tons of cool decisions and things to consider here, but oftentimes I was paralyzed by everything I had to keep track of when planning my moves. The game can reach the point when it gets a bit too overwhelming. That feeling should go away once you go past learning the basics.

The theme is really strong and glues together all the mechanics perfectly. I'm not a big fan of the combat system though, there's nothing interesting about it (and it's very fiddly). Also, the unit production and deployment can be a little hard to manage at times, it's easy to forget about something or make mistakes. 

You just have to know going in that luck (rolling dice and drawing cards) plays a big part in the game. But although the randomness can be frustrating, at the same time it makes the game unpredictable and exciting (you just need the luck factor in games like that).

It's hard to for me to fully appreciate Rebellion as I played it only once and the rules were explanined to me in such a way that I did not understand every tiny detail of the mechanics (and there are many little rules that are easy to forget). I'm guessing that if both players are of equal skill and understand 100% of the rules the game becomes a whole different beast and a true battle of wits. I would love to play it again.

By the way, the game should come with a minimap with a screen for the Imperial player to mark the areas indicated by the Probe cards - keeping track of all the planets is a nightmare and really slows down the game! A simple map with tokens would do the job...

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I like Star Wars. I like Risk-like games. I like games with asymmetry. With Star Wars: Rebellion, we have a game that provides a sense of all these elements.

Rebellion is a sort of "cat and mouse" game where one player (Rebels) is trying to avoid or distract the other (Empire) long enough to run out the clock. Of course, how the cat and mouse play their chase is much more involved than that but if you boil the game down, that's what you've got.

The Rebels are trying to draw attention this way and that. The trick is, if the Empire wants to stop their shenanigans, it will slow down their search. On the other hand, if they don't stop them, the Rebels will speed up their own progress or eat away at the Empires resources.

But make no mistake, the Rebels are very much the "mouse" here...and if they get cornered by the cat, chances are they're gonna be lunch.

This game might be higher on my list but for two inter-related problems. First is it's length. Barring really bad luck on the part of the Rebels, the game will take a minimum of 3 hours and, more likely, 4 or 5 hours to finish. There's nothing objectively wrong with a lengthy game and it's length certainly contributes to a sense of it's epic scope. But this is compounded by it's second problem, which is that it isn't a very satisfying experience for more than 2 players. Yes, the game technically supports 3 or 4 people but it doesn't really do so in any way that's clever.

It also has the misfortune of carrying a combat system that, while competent, isn't all that interesting or rewarding. But it constitutes such a small portion of the experience that I wouldn't really call it a detractor.

So, if you want to play a 2-player, competitive Star Wars saga for an evening, this is the best place to turn.

RATING: 8
First, let me get my butthurt out of the way: don’t play as the Rebels in your first game against an experienced player. Thematically, the Rebels are supposed to feel against the ropes and hopeless, but someone who has played the game before will know that’s more of a feeling than the reality as they figure out ways to punch holes in the Empire’s armor. A new player, however, will feel doubly hopeless and they’re unsure of how to play [i]and[/i] the game is sending them signals that they’re not doing well.

Okay, with that out of the way, the game is really interesting. It’s not at all what I expected when I first saw it. Leaders are really workers and missions are basically the work they’re doing. It’s a worker placement game. At the same time, the Rebellion is trying to hide long enough to achieve their goals while the Empire stomps around the galaxy trying to find and destroy their base. The [i]feel[/i] of the game is just right.

I’m looking forward to playing again and hope my frustrations from the first play go away.

Tentatively recommended.

RATING: 8
Good tactical combat game; excellent thematic game. There's quite a learning curve and the game itself is fairly long, and it's definitely best with two players, so it's not very easily added to a collection unless you like tactical war games. 

While I'm not usually a fan of dice rolling to resolve combat, it didn't feel as arbitrary in this game as it does to me in other games -- maybe a consequence of damage types and tactical cards. The different objectives and play styles of the rebels and the empire makes an interesting game dynamic, and optimum troop movement/deployment is tantamount. 

Definitely recommend for any star wars fan who happens to like tactical games.

RATING: 9
Strategy game with asymmetric player powers which manages to capture the feel of a star wars story in an excellent manner.  This is not a wargame.  The actual conflict on the board, despite the large number of minis, is actually a minor sidebar to the main action.

Pure and simple, this is a hide and seek style game, in which one side (Imperial) is trying to find the objective (rebel base) while the other is attempting to run out the clock (Rebels).  It is a race game, and the leader mechanism is actually reminiscent of WP mechanic.

It is really a fantastic game which owes a great debt to War of the Ring, a comparison which is often cited.  It is like, yet unlike.  However, it does not seem to have the same capacity for replay as WotR.  Although it may not be as good a game as WotR, there are many, many titles for which that same claim can be made, but which are still worth playing.  This is definitely worth playing.

RATING: 8
Finally...the HUGE, non-miniatures, self-contained Star Wars board game you've been waiting for ever since Fantasy Flight got the license. And I'm 99 percent sure it's totally NOT the game you're expecting to get, especially if you go into it thinking it's just a souped-up version of Risk. Way more an abstract version of Star Wars than I thought it would be (your "leaders" just instantly "appear" wherever you need them to, and they are represented by skill icons), learning the game is a bear due to a ruleset larded out enough totally arbitrary rules that I'm sure you'll be playing it wrong for at least your first ten games. Still, it reminds me of old standbys like Arkham horror in that there's something rewarding about leaning into the game's rules and taking your time with it. Sort of a mix of dice challenges, secret missions, hide and seek, and more traditional capture resources and deploy troops stuff, it may not be for everyone, and people who've played it a lot will start to have an advantage (they're more familiar with the decks of possible missions) but still...as THE (current) big Star Wars "event" game, it can't be ignored. Also...the space combat system is just fine as is, by the way. The last thing this game needs is to be made MORE complex in anyway.

RATING: 6
Very long Team-based war game. 2 players per team in a game up to 4 players. 2 people try to find the rebel base; the others try to either hide, or get their influence up as rounds increase.   I am not a war game player; I found it quite interesting in some respects.  I like the Red Alert style "building" timeline, but it didn't really make sense how stuff got into the building timeline before it moves.

However, I didn't like the really long down-time, the really long game play (at least 4-5 hours), I didn't like how fighting took 15mins per player.   I found it very tedious and repetitive.  Also it did feel like a very expensive game of hide and seek.

Maybe Star Wars fans will like this one, but its not for me.

RATING: 8
Epic game that reminds me of a two-player Twilight Imperium. Although I prefer the combat system of the latter, there is plenty of theme in this game and the components are awesome.

RATING: 3
The Salt production in this game is off the charts.

You can't play until you know EVERYTHING in the game, AND have the main strategies of BOTH factions memorized to the point that you can identify them before the first move is taken if possible. because if you didn't you will spend a full turn doing nothing to stop or deter your opponent.

If you are bad at reading people as the empire you will lose. If you have a bad poker face as the rebels you will lose. Both of these situations will have you sitting there for 3-7 HOURS waiting to finally see this happen.

There cards and plays that WILL negate whole rounds of moves you made/make that will occur mostly randomly.

This game is a pain for new players, asking way to much for a frustrating slog of a game that lets your opponent shit in your breakfast.

The Empire of course can instantly win on round one if they accidentally stumble across the base with a fleet movement, and optimal starts where used.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
FFG nailed it with this game. This breathes SW-movies all over and is a joy to play. I love the overwhelming presence of the Imperials yet their despair to find the base. I love the counter-insurgency gameplay of the Rebels making precision strikes and doing destabilizing missions all over the galaxy. This is also a game of leaders. Where to assign them, which mission will they take, which fleet will they command or do they stay on guard to counter the plans of the opponent. It makes for a good amount of decisions in the game. 

The game takes a long time to play but honestly that wasn't an issue since I felt so drawn into the game. Afterwards I can tell a good memorable story about the game just played and that's a big plus. 

On combat: the system works and yes there is luck involved but it's not bad at all. It fits the game. I do love how fleet movement works (Twilight Imperium-like), very clever. 

Played both sides and they seem (this is always a big hurdle for FFG) balanced. It would have been near perfect if rules were having more detailed examples.

RATING: 6
After a few more plays, the length does not come down. It seems like a valid Imperial strategy is to focus many leaders on movement, downplaying missions because they're not as useful. That seems a shame.

---------------

Two plays in. It's fun, and tense. It's not super deep (compared to a heavy strategic wargame), but I think it has more replay value than some think, given that what missions and leaders you see in each game will vary quite a lot, and the game is all about how those play out. There is also a semi-random initial setup (although use the basic fixed one on your first game), which helps a bit.

It's definitely thematic, especially thanks to being so leader-centric; we were frequently joking about what the characters were going through. Combat is also good; just dice-y enough and card-y enough to get some nice slugs in before continuing with the strategic game. The hidden base mechanic is very nice, as is the way the rebel troops can pop out all over the place. The unit production mechanics are very streamlined.

The miniatures are great quality, as are all the components, as expected.

There's not many cards in the Rebel Objectives deck, and it's a tiered deck (I,II,III cards), so the Empire player usually has a pretty good idea what various things the Rebel will be up to. Perhaps if there were more open-ended, constant things that always garnered reputation (how the Rebel runs out the clock), it might feel like there were more options for the Rebel player. But I'm not sure; maybe the alternatives would be too gamey.

The order in which you get your missions and leaders can have a very big effect. Not sure how much this can be countered.

The rules are pretty straightforward, only a few fine-print points, for example the defensive card-play timing in the combat, and some of the rules concerning the Rebel base and captures/rescues.

Coming in pretty long, 4 hours for admittedly slow players, maybe too long for the luck involved, but this might improve as I play with more experienced players and less new players.

4 players might be fun if both players know what they're doing and are invested, but the actual 4-player mechanics feel clunky and unnecessary. 2 player is better, or playing with teams without any actual split in control. This really is a 2 player game.

Knowing the cards/objectives can help a lot in this kind of game. Using the Rebel objectives reference sheet (in the files section) is probably a good idea until you get a feel for what's in there.
